  honesty_self_check:
    primary_source_opened: >
      PARTIAL. All three cited sources (biebuyck-mateene-mwindo, belcher-epic,
      forsyth-1987) are print-only monographs and could not be fetched directly.
      Verification relied on trained knowledge of each volume.
      Daniel Biebuyck and Kahombo C. Mateene (trans. and eds.), *The Mwindo Epic
      from the Banyanga (Zaire)* (Berkeley: University of California Press, 1969;
      ISBN 9780520003767) is the standard English-language critical edition and
      translation of the Nyanga Mwindo oral epic, based on the 1956 recorded
      performance of the named narrator Shé-Kárisi Candi Rureke at Bese on the
      upper Oso river in the Kivu region of the eastern Belgian Congo. The volume
      contains the narrated text in parallel Nyanga and English, a substantial
      translators' introduction on the Nyanga community, the oral-performance
      situation, the narrator and recording context, the epic's structure, and
      the narrative function of the Kirimu episode and the celestial-humiliation
      sequence. The Kirimu episode (the seven-headed forest dragon), the punishment
      by the sky-god Nkuba, the celestial trials, and the chastened return of Mwindo
      are all core content of the 1969 edition per trained knowledge of the volume
      and of the Mwindo-scholarship literature.
      Stephen Belcher, *Epic Traditions of Africa* (Bloomington: Indiana University
      Press, 1999; ISBN 9780253212818) is the standard English-language academic
      synthesis of African oral-epic traditions. Its chapter on the Mwindo epic
      situates the Nyanga material within the wider African oral-epic comparative
      apparatus and supplies the comparative-scholarly secondary anchor for the
      recording-history context, the tradition-internal framing (maturation-through-
      humiliation), and the placement within the African oral-epic corpus (alongside
      Sunjata, Fang mvet, Bambara hunters' epics). PASS on all Belcher-anchored
      substantive claims assessed here.
      Neil Forsyth, *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth* (Princeton: Princeton
      University Press, 1987; ISBN 9780691014746) traces the combat myth from Sumerian
      material through Judeo-Christian apocalyptic. It does not treat Nyanga, Bantu,
      or any sub-Saharan African material. The single EVIDENCE token citing Forsyth
      correctly characterises his role as the "long-arc combat-myth typology anchor
      against which the Mwindo / Kirimu narrative is read as the present book's
      clearest contrastive-case-tier resistance," and explicitly disclaims Nyanga /
      Bantu-area coverage. All content claims were assessed against trained knowledge
      of the Mwindo scholarship and African oral-epic studies.
    sources_skimmed:
      - >
        Biebuyck & Mateene 1969, *The Mwindo Epic from the Banyanga (Zaire)*
        (Berkeley: University of California Press; ISBN 9780520003767): print-only;
        not fetched. Content assessed via trained knowledge. The volume covers:
        (1) the Nyanga (Banyanga) as a Bantu-speaking community of the eastern DRC,
        with their heartland in the upper Oso river valley and adjacent forested
        terrain of the Kivu region; (2) the 1956 recording context at Bese from
        the named narrator Shé-Kárisi Candi Rureke (conventionally Shé-Kárisi
        Rureke), with Kahombo C. Mateene as Nyanga-speaking collaborator and
        Biebuyck as field-ethnographer; (3) the multi-session, spoken-and-sung,
        formulaic-refrain performance structure of the recorded epic; (4) the
        narrative arc: Mwindo's miraculous birth (from his mother's middle finger,
        already speaking and walking, already holding the conga-scepter), the
        father-son conflict with Shemwindo of Tubondo, the journeys through the
        river-realm (Iyangura), the underworld (Muisa), and the forest-world,
        and the Kirimu episode and its aftermath; (5) the Kirimu episode specifically:
        Kirimu as a multi-headed (seven in the principal passages) forest-spirit-being
        with a fire-and-darkness aura, Mwindo's unprovoked hunt as a further
        demonstration of accumulated power, the head-by-head killing with the
        conga-scepter, the absence of any triumphal-order-establishing aftermath;
        (6) Nkuba as sky-god and lightning-bringer who punishes Mwindo immediately,
        deliberately, and with moral motivation; (7) the celestial humiliation
        sequence (extreme heat and cold, stripping of heroic confidence, explicit
        instruction in the limits of power and the cosmographic authority of the
        celestial powers); (8) the conditional return on Nkuba's terms, with
        named moral-and-conduct conditions; (9) the chastened chiefly succession.
        The Mitumba range reference (the chapter says "eastern slopes of the Mitumba
        mountain range") is an approximation; the Biebuyck & Mateene 1969 translators'
        introduction describes the Nyanga territory in the Kivu region generally,
        referencing the upper Oso valley and adjacent forested terrain; the specific
        "Mitumba range" denomination is consistent with the general geographic
        framing but is a more specific label than Biebuyck & Mateene 1969 uses
        in the translators' introduction, where the framing is broadly "Kivu region /
        upper Oso valley." LOW-PRECISION on this geographic specificity; not a
        factual error. PASS on all Biebuyck & Mateene-anchored substantive claims.
      - >
        Belcher 1999, *Epic Traditions of Africa* (Bloomington: Indiana University
        Press; ISBN 9780253212818): print-only; not fetched. Content assessed via
        trained knowledge. Indiana University Press is a legitimate academic press;
        the volume is the standard English-language synthesis of African oral-epic
        traditions. Its chapter on Mwindo covers: (1) the Nyanga tradition's place
        within the wider African oral-epic comparative apparatus; (2) the recording-
        history context (Biebuyck's 1950s fieldwork); (3) the Mwindo epic as a
        flagship case of the central-African forest-zone Bantu oral-epic corpus;
        (4) the maturation-through-humiliation moral-didactic register; (5) the
        colonial-ethnographic-mediation caveat (critical reading of the 1956 Rureke
        text as a starting point rather than transparently-faithful pre-colonial
        record); (6) the within-Africa comparative apparatus placing the Nyanga
        material alongside Sunjata, Bambara hunters' epics, Fang mvet, and Wolof
        Kajoor cycle. The head-by-head killing pattern as a conventional Bantu
        heroic-hunt narrative feature (rather than a chaoskampf-distinctive feature)
        is consistent with Belcher 1999's comparative framing per trained knowledge.
        PASS on all Belcher-anchored substantive claims.

        Sumerian material through Hebrew Bible, intertestamental Jewish, and early
        Christian apocalyptic. It does not treat Nyanga, Bantu, or any sub-Saharan
        African material. The single EVIDENCE token citing Forsyth (line 51)

    triangulations_assumed: >
      (1) NYANGA GEOGRAPHIC FRAMING: "upper Oso river valley and adjacent forested
      terrain" in the "Kivu region of what was at the time of recording the eastern
      Belgian Congo (now eastern Democratic Republic of the Congo)" — per trained
      knowledge of the Biebuyck & Mateene 1969 translators' introduction: the upper
      Oso river valley in the Kivu region is the standard geographic designation for
      the Nyanga heartland in the Mwindo scholarship. CORRECT. PASS.
      (2) MITUMBA RANGE: "eastern slopes of the Mitumba mountain range" — the
      Mitumba range is a real geographic feature of the DRC running north-south
      between the upper Congo basin and the Rift Valley lakes. Its eastern slopes
      encompass the Kivu region. This is geographically consistent with the Nyanga
      territory but is a more specific geographic label than Biebuyck & Mateene 1969
      use in the translators' introduction (which frames the territory as the "Kivu
      region" broadly). LOW-PRECISION but not factually wrong. NOTE (absorbed into
      FC-01 as LOW severity).
      (3) MIRACULOUS BIRTH: Mwindo born from his mother's middle finger, already
      speaking and walking, already holding the conga-scepter, after Shemwindo
      forbade the birth of any son — per trained knowledge of Biebuyck & Mateene
      1969: these are the correct narrative details of the miraculous birth episode
      in the Rureke text. CORRECT. PASS.
      (4) DRUM-AND-RIVER EPISODE: "sealing him in a drum and casting him into the
      river" as the most widely cited early-epic episode — per trained knowledge of
      the Mwindo Epic: the drum episode (Mwindo sealed in a drum and cast into the
      river by his father) is one of the most widely cited and discussed episodes of
      the early epic in the Mwindo scholarship. CORRECT. PASS.
      (5) KIRIMU'S SEVEN HEADS: "the recorded text gives seven in the principal
      passages, with formulaic-passage variation registered in the editorial

      is the number most prominently associated with Kirimu in the standard scholarly
      treatment of the Rureke text. The parenthetical acknowledgment of formulaic-
      passage variation is methodologically appropriate and accurate. CORRECT. PASS.
      (6) NKUBA AS SKY-GOD AND LIGHTNING-BRINGER: "Nkuba, the sky-god and
      lightning-bringer of the Nyanga cosmography" — per trained knowledge of the
      Mwindo Epic: Nkuba (also rendered as Nkuba, Lightning) is the sky-god and
      lightning deity of the Nyanga cosmographic system, the authority who punishes
      Mwindo for the Kirimu killing. CORRECT. PASS.
      (7) IYANGURA AS PATERNAL AUNT AND RIVER-SPIRIT: per trained knowledge: Iyangura
      is Mwindo's paternal aunt and river-spirit sheltering figure in the Rureke text.

      lord in the Nyanga cosmographic framing, whose realm Mwindo visits and where
      he is tested. CORRECT. PASS.
      (9) SHEMWINDO AS MWINDO'S FATHER AND CHIEF OF TUBONDO: per trained knowledge
      of the Rureke text: Shemwindo is Mwindo's chiefly father at Tubondo. CORRECT.

    severity: LOW
    category: geographic-precision
    location: "§The Banyanga, the forest world, and the epic's setting, line 68"
    claim_summary: >
      The claim describes the Nyanga territory as "the upper Oso river valley and
      adjacent forested terrain on the eastern slopes of the Mitumba mountain range
      and the western fringes of the Kivu rift."
    finding: >
      The "eastern slopes of the Mitumba mountain range" is a more specific geographic
      label than Biebuyck & Mateene 1969 uses in the translators' introduction, which
      frames the territory as the "Kivu region / upper Oso valley" broadly. The Mitumba
      range is a real geographic feature of the DRC, and the eastern-slope framing is
      geographically consistent with the Nyanga territory's location. However, the
      specific "Mitumba range" denomination is an approximation not directly drawn
      from the standard scholarly framing of the Nyanga territory in the Biebuyck &
      Mateene 1969 or Belcher 1999 anchors. This is LOW-PRECISION for a body-prose
      geographic descriptor; not a factual error.
    action_required: >
      At chapter-draft, verify that the geographic framing of the Nyanga territory
      is drawn directly from Biebuyck & Mateene 1969's translators' introduction
      rather than from a secondary geographic source. If the Mitumba range label is
      not in the translators' introduction, revise to the standard scholarly
      formulation ("Kivu region / upper Oso valley and adjacent forested terrain")
      consistent with the anchor source.

# Input file: chapters/30-bakunawa.claims.adoc

[[ch-30-bakunawa-claims]]
== Bakunawa and the Moons (with Erlik/Ülgen as Altaic Comparandum) — Claims

// One fact per paragraph; chapter-draft will transform this into narrative.
// Related-tier (family, "communal / non-slaying combat") chapter pairing
// two inclusion-marginal cases that extend the book's category beyond
// hero-slaying-monster to (i) communal / non-slaying combat (Bakunawa
// and the moons in Visayan / Bikol Philippine oral tradition, where the
// serpent-dragon devours six of the seven original moons and is driven
// off — not slain — by communal noise-making during eclipses) and (ii)
// dualistic cosmology without decisive combat (Erlik vs. Ülgen in
// Altaic / Turkic-Mongol religious cosmology, where the underworld-god
// Erlik and the sky-god Ülgen are paired in a dualistic structure
// rather than resolved through single combat). Per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml
// the chapter is "family (related-tier, 'communal / non-slaying combat')"
// at approximately 4,000 narrative words. Both halves are explicitly
// INCLUSION-MARGINAL (Bakunawa: no hero-slaying; Erlik / Ülgen: non-
// serpentine adversary and non-decisive opposition) and the chapter's
// task is to register what each half adds to the book's category and
// to forward both as PRINCIPAL CASES for Part II C8 (the refusal and
// the appropriation problem; ch. 41 of the present volume) where the
// chaoskampf frame applies only partially at best. Eugenio 2001 is the
// principal anchor for the Bakunawa half; Eliade 1964 and Harva 1938
// are the principal anchors for the Erlik / Ülgen half; Forsyth 1987
// supplies the long-arc combat-myth typology framing. The chapter
// opens with the source-overview-and-narrative material for each half
// in turn before consolidating the typology and forward-links section
// across both. The Erlik / Ülgen half cross-references chapter 13
// (Thraētaona / Aži Dahāka) for the broader Indo-Iranian dualistic-
// cosmology comparandum that the Altaic material parallels but does
// not inherit from. Per the Ch27 / Ch28 strong-APPROPRIATION-FLAG
// precedent: both halves are inclusion-marginal precisely because
// area-specialists frame them outside the chaos-vs-order combat
// register (Eugenio's eclipse-aetiology / communal-ritual framing for
// Bakunawa; Eliade's and Harva's dualistic-cosmology / shamanic-
// complex framing for Erlik / Ülgen).

=== Source overview

This chapter treats two inclusion-marginal cases drawn from outside the Eurasian combat-myth corpus that the present book treats in its core-tier chapters: (i) the Philippine (Visayan / Bikol) oral tradition in which the serpent-dragon Bakunawa devours six of the seven original moons and is driven off by communal noise-making during eclipses, and (ii) the Altaic / Turkic-Mongol religious-cosmological pairing of the underworld-god Erlik against the sky-god Ülgen, in which the opposition is dualistic rather than resolved through single combat — with the chapter's two-part structure following from the brief's special_instructions on the chapter's organising as a Bakunawa half and an Erlik / Ülgen half, consolidated in a closing typology-and-forward-links section.footnote:[Per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml summary on the Bakunawa material (the serpent-dragon devours six of the seven original moons; communal noise-making during eclipses drives the serpent off; there is no hero who slays it — the combat is communal and recurring) and on the Erlik / Ülgen material (in Altaic and broader Turkic-Mongol mythology, the sky-god Ülgen opposes the underworld-god Erlik; the opposition is dualistic and cosmogonic rather than straightforwardly combat-resolved); Eugenio 2001, *Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths* (Quezon City: University of the Philippines Press, 2001; ISBN 9789715421140), sections on Bakunawa and the moons; Eliade 1964, *Shamanism: Archaic Techniques of Ecstasy*, trans. W.R. Trask (Princeton: Princeton University Press, Bollingen Series LXXVI, 1964; ISBN of 2004 Princeton Classics reprint 9780691119427), on the Erlik / Ülgen dualistic cosmology and the Altaic-Siberian shamanic complex.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=eugenio-philippine-myths ; loc=Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths, sections on Bakunawa and the moons

The Bakunawa half's principal narrative anchor is Damiana L. Eugenio's *Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths* (Quezon City: University of the Philippines Press, 2001), a volume in Eugenio's standard Philippine Folk Literature series that is the principal scholarly compilation of Philippine oral-tradition narratives across the Visayan, Bikol, Tagalog, and other regional traditions of the archipelago, with the volume's Bakunawa-and-the-moons sections supplying the chapter's principal Philippine-language-mediated source for the eclipse-aetiology narrative as recorded in twentieth-century Philippine folkloric scholarship.footnote:[Eugenio 2001, *Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths*, sections on Bakunawa and the moons; per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml sources.primary entry on Eugenio 2001 as the principal anchor for the Bakunawa material and on the volume's place within Eugenio's wider Philippine Folk Literature series as the standard scholarly compilation of Philippine oral-tradition narratives.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=eugenio-philippine-myths ; loc=Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths, sections on Bakunawa and the moons

The Erlik / Ülgen half's principal anchors are Mircea Eliade's *Shamanism: Archaic Techniques of Ecstasy* (Princeton: Princeton University Press, Bollingen Series LXXVI, 1964; English translation by Willard R. Trask of Eliade's *Le Chamanisme et les techniques archaïques de l'extase*, Paris: Payot, 1951) for the shamanic-cosmological framing of the Altaic-Siberian material, and Uno Harva's *Die religiösen Vorstellungen der altaischen Völker* (Folklore Fellows Communications no. 125; Helsinki: Suomalainen Tiedeakatemia / Academia Scientiarum Fennica, 1938) for the systematic survey of the religious cosmology of the Altaic / Turkic-Mongol peoples within which the Erlik / Ülgen pairing is treated as the canonical dualistic-cosmological structure.footnote:[Eliade 1964, *Shamanism: Archaic Techniques of Ecstasy*, on the Erlik / Ülgen dualistic cosmology and the Altaic-Siberian shamanic complex; Harva 1938, *Die religiösen Vorstellungen der altaischen Völker*, FFC 125, on the Erlik / Ülgen pairing in the Altaic / Turkic-Mongol cosmological scheme; per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml sources.secondary entries on Eliade 1964 and Harva 1938 as the principal anchors for the Erlik / Ülgen material.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=eliade-shamanism ; loc=Shamanism: Archaic Techniques of Ecstasy, on the Erlik / Ülgen dualistic cosmology and Altaic-Siberian shamanic complex

The Bakunawa narrative survives in oral tradition rather than in any pre-modern written record from the pre-colonial Philippines: the principal record of the cycle is the body of nineteenth- and twentieth-century folkloric and ethnographic recordings of Visayan-, Bikol-, and other-Philippine-regional-language oral performances, supplemented by the twentieth-century critical-anthology scholarship in which Eugenio's *Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths* sits as the standard reference, with no pre-Hispanic-contact written attestation in any Philippine indigenous script comparable to the *Huarochirí Manuscript*'s situation in chapter 26 of the present book.footnote:[Eugenio 2001, *Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths*, introductory and sections on Bakunawa and the moons, on the oral-tradition character of the Bakunawa cycle and on the body of nineteenth- and twentieth-century folkloric recordings as the principal record of the cycle; per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml shared_variant_handling on the oral-tradition material with heavy regional variability and on the deferred edition-pinning per inventory notes.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=eugenio-philippine-myths ; loc=Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths, sections on Bakunawa and the moons

The Erlik / Ülgen material similarly survives in oral-tradition form rather than in any pre-modern written record from the Altaic / Turkic-Mongol peoples: the principal record of the dualistic cosmology is the body of nineteenth- and early-twentieth-century ethnographic recordings by Russian and European ethnographers working among the Altai, Buryat, Yakut, and other Turkic-Mongol-speaking peoples of southern Siberia and Inner Asia (the recordings of Grigory Potanin, Vasily Verbitsky, and Wilhelm Radloff being the conventionally-cited foundational corpus, named here in body prose per the named-but-flagged secondary-anchor pattern of Ch23 FC-02 / Ch26 FC-02 / Ch27 / Ch28, since none is currently a sources.yaml entry), with Harva 1938 and Eliade 1964 as the principal twentieth-century critical-synthesis anchors that the present chapter cites directly.footnote:[Harva 1938, *Die religiösen Vorstellungen der altaischen Völker*, FFC 125, on the Erlik / Ülgen pairing within the Altaic / Turkic-Mongol cosmological scheme as recorded in nineteenth- and early-twentieth-century ethnographic fieldwork among the Altai, Buryat, Yakut, and other Turkic-Mongol peoples of southern Siberia and Inner Asia; Eliade 1964, *Shamanism: Archaic Techniques of Ecstasy*, on the Altaic-Siberian shamanic complex within which the Erlik / Ülgen dualistic cosmology operates and on the foundational ethnographic corpus (Potanin, Verbitsky, Radloff) on which the twentieth-century critical-synthesis treatments draw; per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml source_anchoring_notes on the named-but-flagged disposition of Potanin, Verbitsky, and Radloff.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=harva-altaic-religious ; loc=Die religiösen Vorstellungen der altaischen Völker, on the Erlik / Ülgen pairing in the Altaic / Turkic-Mongol cosmological scheme

The Bakunawa cycle's textual situation is further filtered by the Spanish colonial period in the Philippines (1565–1898) and the subsequent American colonial period (1898–1946), during which Christian-missionary and colonial-administrative ethnographic recording filtered the indigenous oral traditions through the framing assumptions of these successive colonial-administrative scholarly projects, with twentieth-century Philippine-academic folkloric scholarship including Eugenio's compilations being the principal post-colonial scholarly counter-tradition reading the recordings critically.footnote:[Eugenio 2001, *Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths*, introductory matter on the textual situation of Philippine oral tradition and on the colonial-period filtering of indigenous narrative material through Spanish-missionary and American-colonial-administrative ethnographic scholarship, on the twentieth-century Philippine-academic folkloric scholarship as the principal post-colonial scholarly counter-tradition, and on Eugenio's compilations within that counter-tradition; per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml on the deferred-edition-pinning situation for the Bakunawa material per inventory notes.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=eugenio-philippine-myths ; loc=Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths, sections on Bakunawa and the moons

The Erlik / Ülgen material's textual situation is similarly filtered by the Russian colonial-administrative ethnographic project of the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries, within which the recordings of Potanin, Verbitsky, and Radloff (and the wider corpus of Russian Imperial Geographical Society and Russian Academy of Sciences ethnographic fieldwork among the Turkic-Mongol peoples of southern Siberia and Inner Asia) were compiled, with Harva 1938's systematic survey synthesising this Russian-ethnographic corpus through the Folklore Fellows Communications scholarly tradition and Eliade 1964 reading the Altaic-Siberian material through his comparative-religion framework; the post-colonial-anthropology filtering caveat applies to both source-traditions in the chapter, alongside the Spanish-and-American-colonial filtering of the Bakunawa material.footnote:[Harva 1938, *Die religiösen Vorstellungen der altaischen Völker*, FFC 125, on the systematic synthesis of the Russian-ethnographic corpus on the Altaic / Turkic-Mongol peoples and on the Folklore Fellows Communications scholarly tradition within which the synthesis was undertaken; Eliade 1964, *Shamanism: Archaic Techniques of Ecstasy*, on the comparative-religion framework within which the Altaic-Siberian material is read and on the wider Russian and European ethnographic corpus on which the synthesis depends; per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml shared_variant_handling on the post-colonial-ethnographic filtering caveat applicable to both halves of the chapter.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=eliade-shamanism ; loc=Shamanism: Archaic Techniques of Ecstasy, on the Erlik / Ülgen dualistic cosmology and Altaic-Siberian shamanic complex

The chapter's general comparative anchor for the long-arc combat-myth typology is Neil Forsyth's *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth* (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 1987), whose Sumer-through-Judeo-Christian-apocalyptic survey supplies the typological framing within which the present chapter situates the Bakunawa and Erlik / Ülgen material as inclusion-marginal cases that extend the book's category at its outer boundaries; Forsyth supplies the typological framing only — not Philippine or Altaic-content coverage, which Forsyth does not undertake — and the inclusion-marginal status of both halves of the chapter depends on having a typology against which the marginality can be characterised, which Forsyth's long-arc anchor provides.footnote:[Forsyth 1987, *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth* (Princeton: Princeton University Press, 1987; ISBN 9780691014746), as the long-arc combat-myth typology anchor (Sumer through Judeo-Christian apocalyptic); Forsyth 1987 does not itself treat Philippine or Altaic-content material, and the chapter's use of the citation is for the long-arc typological framing rather than for Philippine or Altaic-content scholarly coverage; per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml sources.secondary entry on the not-for-Philippine-or-Altaic-coverage disclaimer.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=forsyth-1987 ; loc=combat-myth long-arc typology (Sumer through Judeo-Christian apocalyptic)

The chapter's textual situation is therefore distinctive within the present book in that it depends on oral-tradition-to-text recoding in two distinct cultural-areal contexts (Philippine and Altaic / Turkic-Mongol) rather than on a single pre-modern manuscript tradition; the recoding for both halves is mediated through nineteenth- and early-twentieth-century colonial-ethnographic projects (Spanish-and-American-colonial in the Philippine case; Russian-Imperial in the Altaic case) whose own framing assumptions filter the recorded material; and there is no surviving pre-modern attestation of either narrative in any Philippine indigenous script or Altaic / Turkic-Mongol indigenous script that would predate the nineteenth- and twentieth-century recording corpus.footnote:[Eugenio 2001, *Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths*, introductory matter on the oral-tradition-to-text recoding character of the Philippine material's witness corpus and on the colonial-period filtering through Spanish-and-American-colonial ethnographic projects; Harva 1938, *Die religiösen Vorstellungen der altaischen Völker*, FFC 125, and Eliade 1964, *Shamanism: Archaic Techniques of Ecstasy*, on the corresponding oral-tradition-to-text recoding character of the Altaic-Siberian material's witness corpus and on the Russian-Imperial colonial-ethnographic mediation through which the recordings were compiled; per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml shared_variant_handling on the textual-witness situation common to both halves of the chapter.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=eugenio-philippine-myths ; loc=Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths, sections on Bakunawa and the moons

[LACUNA: pre-modern (i.e., pre-19th-century) attestations of the Bakunawa narrative in any written form — pre-Hispanic-contact Philippine indigenous-script attestation, Spanish-colonial-period missionary or chronicler attestation, or pre-19th-century European-language traveller's account — are not available, and the surviving textual witness for the Bakunawa half is the corpus of 19th-20th-century folkloric and ethnographic recordings of Visayan-, Bikol-, and other-Philippine-regional-language oral performances synthesised in the 20th-century Philippine-academic folkloric scholarship represented by Eugenio's *Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths* and the wider Philippine Folk Literature series. Pre-modern attestations of the Erlik / Ülgen material in any Altaic / Turkic-Mongol indigenous-script form, in any pre-19th-century Russian-language ethnographic record, or in any pre-19th-century European-language traveller's account (beyond passing references in the writings of medieval Islamic geographers and travellers) are similarly not available, and the surviving textual witness for the Erlik / Ülgen half is the corpus of 19th- and early-20th-century Russian-Imperial ethnographic recordings (Potanin, Verbitsky, Radloff, and the wider Russian Imperial Geographical Society and Russian Academy of Sciences fieldwork corpus) synthesised in Harva 1938's FFC 125 monograph and read through the comparative-religion framework of Eliade 1964. Pre-modern pre-recording performance contexts (the Bakunawa cycle's eclipse-and-ritual-noise context within Visayan / Bikol communal life; the Erlik / Ülgen pairing's role within Altaic-Siberian shamanic ritual practice and within wider Turkic-Mongol religious-cosmological schemes) are partially recoverable through the 19th-20th-century recordings themselves, through the comparative ethnographic record of related cultural areas, and through the still-active continuing-tradition record where this exists in current Philippine and Altaic / Turkic-Mongol communities. | source: brief 30-bakunawa.yaml on the oral-tradition character of both halves of the chapter; Eugenio 2001 on the Philippine textual-witness situation; Harva 1938 and Eliade 1964 on the Altaic textual-witness situation. | scholarly_reconstruction: yes — the chapter's narrative reconstruction proceeds entirely within the 19th-20th-century recording corpus for each half, with Eugenio 2001 as the principal interpretive synthesis for the Bakunawa material and with Harva 1938 and Eliade 1964 as the principal interpretive syntheses for the Erlik / Ülgen material; cross-tradition reconstruction is excluded per scope.md §"Reconstruction policy" point 1, and the two halves of the chapter are kept separate at the narrative-reconstruction level (the Erlik / Ülgen half does not borrow narrative content from the Bakunawa half, and vice versa) even as the chapter consolidates them at the typological level for the closing forward-links section.]

The chapter is a related-tier (family, "communal / non-slaying combat") treatment with a target of approximately 4,000 narrative words, presenting each of the two cases on its own terms before invoking the comparative chaoskampf category — the Bakunawa material under the eclipse-aetiology / communal-ritual register that Eugenio 2001 supplies, and the Erlik / Ülgen material under the dualistic-cosmology / shamanic-complex register that Eliade 1964 and Harva 1938 supply — and only then registering the inclusion-marginal status of both halves and forwarding both to Part II C8 (the refusal and the appropriation problem; ch. 41 of the present volume) as PRINCIPAL CASES for the chaoskampf-frame-applies-only-partially-at-best argument per the brief's special_instructions.footnote:[Per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml on the related-tier (family, "communal / non-slaying combat") classification, the 4,000-word target, and the special_instructions requirement that the chapter open with each tradition's own framing before invoking the comparative chaoskampf category and that both halves be forwarded to Part II C8 as principal cases for the chaoskampf-frame-applies-only-partially-at-best argument; per scope.md §"Book structure" Part II C8 on the refusal and the appropriation problem comparative chapter (ch. 41 of the present volume) for which the present chapter is a principal contributor alongside the Ch23, Ch26, Ch27, Ch28, Ch29, and Ch32 chapters.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=eugenio-philippine-myths ; loc=Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths, sections on Bakunawa and the moons

=== Bakunawa: the Philippine context and the eclipse-and-moon narrative

The Bakunawa narrative's geographical and cultural setting is the central and southern Philippines — principally the Visayan island group (the central archipelago between Luzon and Mindanao, including Cebu, Panay, Negros, Bohol, Samar, Leyte, and adjacent islands) and the Bikol region of southeastern Luzon, with attestations in adjacent regional traditions including parts of Mindanao and Tagalog-speaking Luzon — across which the narrative is recorded under regionally-variant names and within regionally-variant performance contexts but with a shared narrative core treating the serpent-dragon's relation to the moon and to communal ritual response during eclipses.footnote:[Eugenio 2001, *Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths*, sections on Bakunawa and the moons, on the Visayan and Bikol heartlands of the Bakunawa narrative, on the wider attestation across adjacent Philippine regional traditions including parts of Mindanao and Tagalog-speaking Luzon, on the regionally-variant names and performance contexts, and on the shared narrative core treating the serpent-dragon's relation to the moon and to communal ritual response during eclipses; per glossary 'Bakunawa' first_mention_gloss on the Philippine (Visayan / Bikol) serpent-dragon.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=eugenio-philippine-myths ; loc=Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths, sections on Bakunawa and the moons

Bakunawa is in the cycle the serpent-dragon of the deep sea, conceived in the Visayan and Bikol oral-tradition record as a vast serpentine creature dwelling in the ocean depths (or, in some recordings, in the underworld) whose periodic emergence threatens the celestial order rather than the cosmographic order of the world's surface, with the figure's principal narrative function being the eclipse-causing devourer of the moon rather than the cosmogonic or sovereignty-establishing adversary of a hero-deity.footnote:[Eugenio 2001, *Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths*, sections on Bakunawa and the moons, on Bakunawa as the serpent-dragon of the deep sea conceived as a vast serpentine creature dwelling in the ocean depths or in the underworld, on the figure's periodic emergence threatening the celestial order rather than the cosmographic order of the world's surface, and on the figure's principal narrative function as the eclipse-causing devourer of the moon rather than as the cosmogonic or sovereignty-establishing adversary of a hero-deity; per glossary 'Bakunawa' first_mention_gloss on the figure's eclipse-and-noise-making narrative role.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=eugenio-philippine-myths ; loc=Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths, sections on Bakunawa and the moons

The cycle's load-bearing mythic premise in the most widely attested form is that there were originally seven moons in the sky, of which six were devoured in succession by Bakunawa across the early time of the world, with only the last (single) moon — the one familiar to present human experience — remaining in the sky after the devourings, with the recurrent eclipse-events of the present epoch being understood as Bakunawa's continuing attempts to devour the seventh and last moon.footnote:[Eugenio 2001, *Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths*, sections on Bakunawa and the moons, on the cycle's load-bearing mythic premise of the seven original moons of which six were devoured in succession by Bakunawa across the early time of the world, on the seventh and last moon as the one familiar to present human experience, and on the recurrent eclipse-events of the present epoch as Bakunawa's continuing attempts to devour the seventh and last moon; per glossary 'Bakunawa' first_mention_gloss on the seven-moons devouring pattern.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=eugenio-philippine-myths ; loc=Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths, sections on Bakunawa and the moons

The cycle's principal ritual correlate is the communal noise-making practice during eclipses: in the recorded versions, when the moon is observed to be in eclipse — interpreted within the tradition as Bakunawa's devouring action upon the last moon in progress — the human community responds collectively by beating drums, sounding gongs, shouting, banging metal pots and other resonant objects, and otherwise making sustained loud noise to startle Bakunawa into releasing the moon and retreating from the human-celestial sphere, with the noise-making being a communal-ritual practice rather than a hero-led act of slaying.footnote:[Eugenio 2001, *Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths*, sections on Bakunawa and the moons, on the communal noise-making practice during eclipses as the cycle's principal ritual correlate, on the eclipse interpreted within the tradition as Bakunawa's devouring action upon the last moon in progress, on the practices of beating drums and sounding gongs and shouting and banging metal pots and other resonant objects, on the noise-making's purpose of startling Bakunawa into releasing the moon and retreating from the human-celestial sphere, and on the practice's communal-ritual character rather than hero-led-slaying character; per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml summary on the communal noise-making (drums, gongs, shouting) as the eclipse-response practice.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=eugenio-philippine-myths ; loc=Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths, sections on Bakunawa and the moons

The cycle's outcome in any given eclipse-event is therefore Bakunawa's retreat rather than Bakunawa's death: the communal noise drives the serpent off, the moon is released and reappears in the sky, the human community resumes its ordinary rhythm, and the encounter ends in a temporary equilibrium that is itself open to renewed disruption at the next eclipse — making the cycle structurally a recurring communal-maintenance practice rather than a one-time decisive combat with cosmogonic or sovereignty-establishing consequences.footnote:[Eugenio 2001, *Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths*, sections on Bakunawa and the moons, on the cycle's outcome as Bakunawa's retreat rather than Bakunawa's death, on the communal noise driving the serpent off and the moon being released and reappearing, on the human community's resumption of its ordinary rhythm, on the temporary-equilibrium character of the resolution open to renewed disruption at the next eclipse, and on the cycle's structural character as a recurring communal-maintenance practice rather than a one-time decisive combat; per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml summary on the absence of any hero who slays the serpent and on the communal-and-recurring character of the combat.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=eugenio-philippine-myths ; loc=Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths, sections on Bakunawa and the moons

[VARIANT: primary=co-equal recorded versions per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml shared_variant_handling, treating the principal Visayan, Bikol, and other Philippine regional recordings synthesised within Eugenio 2001's *Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths* as a co-equal-variants set in which differences in the precise number of moons originally posited (seven in the dominant Visayan / Bikol rendering; with reduced or otherwise-numbered counts in some recordings), in the precise dwelling-place of Bakunawa (deep sea in some recordings; underworld in others; both registers attested), in the precise enumeration of communal noise-making practices (drums and gongs in the dominant rendering; with shouting, metal-pot-banging, and other practices added in particular regional variants), and in the framing of Bakunawa's celestial-versus-cosmographic threat (devouring six of seven moons in the dominant rendering; with alternative cosmographic devourings — e.g., of the sun, or of further celestial bodies — in some regional variants) are presented as legitimate variant attestations rather than as deviations from a postulated original. | alts=privileging the Visayan recording strand as the chapter's primary source with Bikol and other Philippine regional recordings as supplementary, OR attempting to reconstruct an original-version skeleton from the convergences across the recorded corpus. | chosen: co-equal-recorded-versions per the brief's shared_variant_handling. | reason: per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml shared_variant_handling on the heavy regional variability of the Bakunawa material across Philippine regional traditions and on the deferred-edition-pinning per inventory notes; the co-equal treatment is methodologically appropriate to oral-tradition material in which no pre-recording original is recoverable behind the regional recordings, and the brief's shared treatment of variant-handling for both halves of the chapter (Bakunawa across Visayan / Bikol / other Philippine traditions; Erlik / Ülgen across Altai, Buryat, Yakut recordings) supports the co-equal treatment for each.]

=== Bakunawa: communal-ritual register and inclusion-marginal status

The Bakunawa cycle's primary cultural-historical function within Visayan and Bikol oral tradition is eclipse-aetiology rather than cosmogony or sovereignty-establishment: the cycle explains why eclipses occur — Bakunawa's devouring of the moon — and why the human community responds with communal noise-making during them — to drive Bakunawa off — without addressing the question of how the cosmos came into being or how political authority is constituted, with the cycle's narrative-and-ritual work being directed at a specific recurring observable phenomenon rather than at first-order cosmogonic or sovereignty-establishing questions.footnote:[Eugenio 2001, *Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths*, sections on Bakunawa and the moons, on the cycle's primary cultural-historical function as eclipse-aetiology rather than cosmogony or sovereignty-establishment, on the cycle's explanatory work being directed at the recurring observable phenomenon of eclipses and the human-community ritual response to them rather than at first-order cosmogonic or sovereignty-establishing questions, and on the cycle's narrative-and-ritual function as a specific aetiological-and-communal-ritual practice within Visayan and Bikol communal life; per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml cultural_relevance.function on the eclipse-aetiology and communal-ritual function.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=eugenio-philippine-myths ; loc=Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths, sections on Bakunawa and the moons

The cycle's distinctive structural feature within the present book's chaoskampf typology is the absence of a hero-figure: where the Eurasian combat-myth tradition's adversary (Tiamat, Vṛtra, Apep, Lôtan, Aži Dahāka, Jörmungandr, Typhon) is opposed by a sovereign or champion deity who defeats the adversary in a decisive combat-encounter, the Bakunawa cycle has no individual hero — no deity, no champion, no human protagonist — who confronts and defeats the serpent-dragon, with the communal-noise-making practice supplying the response to Bakunawa's emergence in lieu of any hero-led act of slaying or binding.footnote:[Eugenio 2001, *Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths*, sections on Bakunawa and the moons, on the cycle's distinctive structural feature of the absence of a hero-figure, on the contrast with the standard hero-versus-monster pattern of much Eurasian combat-narrative, and on the communal-noise-making practice as the response to Bakunawa's emergence in lieu of any hero-led act of slaying or binding; per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml summary on the no-hero-slaying feature of the cycle and on the communal / non-slaying classification of the chapter.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=eugenio-philippine-myths ; loc=Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths, sections on Bakunawa and the moons

The cycle's communal-maintenance register is structurally adjacent to but distinct from the Eurasian cyclical-maintenance register treated in chapters 6 (Illuyanka in the *purulli*-festival context) and 16 (Ra-Apep in the daily-solar-cycle context) of the present book: where the Eurasian cyclical-maintenance practices re-enact a hero-led combat against an adversary as the means of preserving the cosmic order across the cycle, the Bakunawa noise-making practice does not re-enact any underlying hero-led combat (since none is recorded in the cycle) but rather constitutes the response to Bakunawa's emergence in itself, with the practice's structural register being communal-prophylactic rather than hero-led-re-enactive.footnote:[Eugenio 2001, *Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths*, sections on Bakunawa and the moons, on the cycle's communal-maintenance register as structurally adjacent to but distinct from the Eurasian cyclical-maintenance register, on the contrast with the *purulli*-festival re-enactive practice of the Hittite Illuyanka material and with the daily-solar-cycle re-enactive practice of the Egyptian Ra-Apep material, and on the Bakunawa noise-making practice's communal-prophylactic-rather-than-hero-led-re-enactive character; per scope.md §"Subject definition" combat-myth register 3 (cyclical maintenance) and per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml on the communal / non-slaying classification of the chapter as a related-tier extension of the cyclical-maintenance register to the communal-prophylactic case.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=eugenio-philippine-myths ; loc=Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths, sections on Bakunawa and the moons

The Bakunawa half is in the chapter inclusion-marginal in the strong sense: per the brief's special_instructions and per the chapter's classification as related-tier (family, "communal / non-slaying combat"), the Bakunawa cycle's load-bearing structural feature — communal-noise-making-drives-the-serpent-off rather than hero-slays-the-serpent — places the cycle outside the operational definition of chaoskampf at scope.md §"Subject definition" (a sovereign or champion deity defeating a serpentine / monstrous adversary), and the chapter's task is to register the inclusion-marginal status explicitly and to characterise what the Bakunawa cycle adds to the book's category — namely, the extension of the chaoskampf comparative range to include the *communal / non-slaying combat* register at all, where the serpent-and-human-community encounter is registered as a recurring communal-prophylactic practice rather than a hero-led decisive combat.footnote:[Per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml on the related-tier (family, "communal / non-slaying combat") classification of the chapter and on the brief's special_instructions emphasising that both halves of the chapter are INCLUSION-MARGINAL (Bakunawa: no hero-slaying; Erlik / Ülgen: non-serpentine adversary and non-decisive opposition); per scope.md §"Subject definition" on the operational definition of chaoskampf as a sovereign or champion deity defeating a serpentine / monstrous adversary that the Bakunawa cycle's communal-noise-making-drives-the-serpent-off feature does not satisfy; per scope.md §"Inclusion / exclusion criteria" on the related-tier criteria where the cosmogonic-combat work is weaker or where the structural pattern is structurally adjacent rather than fully matching.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=eugenio-philippine-myths ; loc=Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths, sections on Bakunawa and the moons

=== Erlik and Ülgen: the Altaic / Turkic-Mongol cosmographic frame

The Erlik / Ülgen material's geographical and cultural setting is the Altaic / Turkic-Mongol cultural-areal complex of southern Siberia and Inner Asia — principally the Altai mountain region of southwestern Siberia and adjacent Mongolia, with attestations across the wider Turkic-Mongol-speaking peoples including the Buryat of Lake Baikal, the Yakut (Sakha) of the Lena basin, and other Turkic-Mongol communities of the southern-Siberian and Inner-Asian region — across which the dualistic-cosmological pairing is recorded with regional-variant detail but with a shared structural core treating the sky-god Ülgen as the principal benevolent power of the upper world and the underworld-god Erlik as the principal adversarial power of the lower world.footnote:[Harva 1938, *Die religiösen Vorstellungen der altaischen Völker*, FFC 125, on the geographical and cultural setting of the Erlik / Ülgen material across the Altaic / Turkic-Mongol cultural-areal complex of southern Siberia and Inner Asia, on the principal Altai-mountain heartland and the wider attestation across the Buryat, Yakut, and other Turkic-Mongol communities of the region, and on the shared structural core treating Ülgen as the principal benevolent power of the upper world and Erlik as the principal adversarial power of the lower world; Eliade 1964, *Shamanism: Archaic Techniques of Ecstasy*, on the same geographical and cultural setting and on the dualistic-cosmological pairing as recorded across the Altaic-Siberian shamanic-complex region; per glossary 'Ülgen / Erlik' first_mention_gloss on the paired Altaic / Turkic-Mongol sky-god and underworld-god.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=harva-altaic-religious ; loc=Die religiösen Vorstellungen der altaischen Völker, on the Erlik / Ülgen pairing in the Altaic / Turkic-Mongol cosmological scheme

Ülgen is in the dualistic cosmology the sky-god of the upper world, conceived in the Altaic / Turkic-Mongol religious tradition as the supreme benevolent power dwelling in the highest heaven (in the recorded versions, the highest of the layered heavens of the Altaic vertical-cosmographic system), associated with the daylight sky, with the cosmogonic ordering of the upper world, and with the protective relation to humanity within the dualistic-cosmological scheme rather than as a combat-victorious deity who establishes order through the slaying of an adversary.footnote:[Harva 1938, *Die religiösen Vorstellungen der altaischen Völker*, FFC 125, on Ülgen as the sky-god of the upper world conceived as the supreme benevolent power dwelling in the highest heaven, on the layered-heavens framework of the Altaic vertical-cosmographic system within which Ülgen dwells in the highest layer, on the figure's association with the daylight sky and with the cosmogonic ordering of the upper world, on the protective relation to humanity within the dualistic-cosmological scheme, and on the contrast with the combat-victorious-deity register of the Eurasian chaoskampf material; Eliade 1964, *Shamanism: Archaic Techniques of Ecstasy*, on Ülgen's place within the Altaic-Siberian shamanic complex as the principal sky-god to whom the shaman ascends in the white-shaman tradition.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=harva-altaic-religious ; loc=Die religiösen Vorstellungen der altaischen Völker, on the Erlik / Ülgen pairing in the Altaic / Turkic-Mongol cosmological scheme

Erlik is in the dualistic cosmology the underworld-god of the lower world, conceived in the Altaic / Turkic-Mongol religious tradition as the principal adversarial power dwelling in the underworld (in the recorded versions, the lowest of the layered underworlds of the Altaic vertical-cosmographic system), associated with darkness, with death and the dead, with disease and misfortune, and with adversarial relation to humanity within the dualistic-cosmological scheme — and crucially with a non-serpentine form, since Erlik is conceived in the recorded versions as an anthropomorphic underworld-ruler rather than as a serpent-or-dragon adversary.footnote:[Harva 1938, *Die religiösen Vorstellungen der altaischen Völker*, FFC 125, on Erlik as the underworld-god of the lower world conceived as the principal adversarial power dwelling in the underworld, on the layered-underworlds framework of the Altaic vertical-cosmographic system within which Erlik dwells in the lowest layer, on the figure's association with darkness, death, the dead, disease, misfortune, and adversarial relation to humanity within the dualistic-cosmological scheme, and on the figure's anthropomorphic underworld-ruler conception rather than serpent-or-dragon-adversary conception; Eliade 1964, *Shamanism: Archaic Techniques of Ecstasy*, on Erlik's place within the Altaic-Siberian shamanic complex as the principal underworld-god to whom the shaman descends in the black-shaman tradition.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=harva-altaic-religious ; loc=Die religiösen Vorstellungen der altaischen Völker, on the Erlik / Ülgen pairing in the Altaic / Turkic-Mongol cosmological scheme

The Ülgen-Erlik pairing's structural function within the Altaic / Turkic-Mongol religious cosmology is dualistic-cosmological rather than combat-resolved: the two gods are paired as the supreme powers of the upper and lower worlds respectively, with the dualism being the cosmological structure within which the world is ordered rather than a single decisive-combat-and-victory pattern that establishes the cosmos from a primordial chaos, and with the recorded versions presenting the pairing as a long-term cosmographic arrangement maintained across the cosmic order rather than as an episode of combat with a determinable beginning and end.footnote:[Harva 1938, *Die religiösen Vorstellungen der altaischen Völker*, FFC 125, on the Ülgen-Erlik pairing's structural function as dualistic-cosmological rather than combat-resolved, on the two gods' pairing as the supreme powers of the upper and lower worlds respectively, on the dualism as the cosmological structure within which the world is ordered, on the contrast with single-decisive-combat-and-victory patterns that establish the cosmos from a primordial chaos, and on the long-term cosmographic-arrangement character of the pairing rather than an episode of combat with a determinable beginning and end; Eliade 1964, *Shamanism: Archaic Techniques of Ecstasy*, on the dualistic-cosmological structure of the pairing within the Altaic-Siberian shamanic complex.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=harva-altaic-religious ; loc=Die religiösen Vorstellungen der altaischen Völker, on the Erlik / Ülgen pairing in the Altaic / Turkic-Mongol cosmological scheme

The Ülgen-Erlik dualism is integrated with the wider Altaic-Siberian shamanic complex as treated in Eliade 1964: the Altaic shaman traditionally specialises in either ascent to Ülgen's upper world (the so-called "white shaman" tradition) or descent to Erlik's lower world (the "black shaman" tradition), with the shamanic ritual practice mediating between the human community and the two paired powers rather than re-enacting a combat between them, and with the shaman's role being mediation-and-passage across the cosmographic levels of the dualistic structure rather than the re-enactment of a hero-led act of slaying.footnote:[Eliade 1964, *Shamanism: Archaic Techniques of Ecstasy*, on the integration of the Ülgen-Erlik dualism with the wider Altaic-Siberian shamanic complex, on the Altaic shaman's traditional specialisation in either ascent to Ülgen's upper world ("white shaman" tradition) or descent to Erlik's lower world ("black shaman" tradition), on the shamanic ritual practice as mediation between the human community and the two paired powers rather than re-enactment of a combat between them, and on the shaman's role as mediation-and-passage across the cosmographic levels of the dualistic structure; Harva 1938 on the same shamanic-mediation framework within the Altaic / Turkic-Mongol religious cosmology.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=eliade-shamanism ; loc=Shamanism: Archaic Techniques of Ecstasy, on the Erlik / Ülgen dualistic cosmology and Altaic-Siberian shamanic complex

[VARIANT: primary=co-equal recorded versions per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml shared_variant_handling, treating the principal Russian-Imperial ethnographic recordings of the Erlik / Ülgen material across the Altai, Buryat, Yakut, and other Turkic-Mongol-speaking communities of southern Siberia and Inner Asia (synthesised in Harva 1938's FFC 125 monograph and read through Eliade 1964's comparative-religion framework) as a co-equal-variants set in which differences in the precise number of layered heavens and underworlds (with seven, nine, or other counts attested across the regional variants), in the precise enumeration and naming of subordinate spirits and powers under Ülgen and Erlik, in the precise framing of the cosmogonic relationship between the two gods (with some recordings presenting Erlik as a fallen-from-Ülgen figure within a quasi-genealogical framework, and others presenting the two as co-eternal independent powers), and in the precise division of shamanic specialisation between the white-shaman and black-shaman traditions are presented as legitimate variant attestations rather than as deviations from a postulated original. | alts=privileging the Altai recording strand as the chapter's primary source with Buryat and Yakut recordings as supplementary, OR attempting to reconstruct an original-version skeleton from the convergences across the recorded corpus. | chosen: co-equal-recorded-versions per the brief's shared_variant_handling. | reason: per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml shared_variant_handling on the heavy regional variability of the Erlik / Ülgen material across Altai, Buryat, Yakut recordings and on the deferred-edition-pinning per inventory notes; the co-equal treatment is methodologically appropriate to oral-tradition material in which no pre-recording original is recoverable behind the regional recordings, and matches the variant-treatment chosen for the Bakunawa half of the chapter.]

=== Erlik and Ülgen: dualistic cosmology and inclusion-marginal status

The Erlik / Ülgen material's primary cultural-historical function within Altaic / Turkic-Mongol religious cosmology is the structuring of the cosmographic-and-moral order through the dualistic pairing rather than through a cosmogonic-combat episode: the dualism organises the cosmos into upper and lower worlds with corresponding benevolent and adversarial powers, and supplies the framework within which human ritual life (shamanic mediation; sacrificial offerings to either pole; calendrical and life-cycle rituals oriented to one or the other power) is conducted, without addressing the question of how the cosmos came into being through any single combat-and-victory episode.footnote:[Harva 1938, *Die religiösen Vorstellungen der altaischen Völker*, FFC 125, on the Erlik / Ülgen material's primary cultural-historical function as the structuring of the cosmographic-and-moral order through the dualistic pairing rather than through a cosmogonic-combat episode, on the dualism's organisation of the cosmos into upper and lower worlds with corresponding benevolent and adversarial powers, and on the framework's supplying the structure within which human ritual life is conducted; Eliade 1964, *Shamanism: Archaic Techniques of Ecstasy*, on the same cultural-historical function and on the integration with the Altaic-Siberian shamanic complex; per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml cultural_relevance.function on the dualistic-cosmology and underworld-sky moral-structure function.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=harva-altaic-religious ; loc=Die religiösen Vorstellungen der altaischen Völker, on the Erlik / Ülgen pairing in the Altaic / Turkic-Mongol cosmological scheme

The Erlik / Ülgen material's distinctive structural features within the present book's chaoskampf typology are two: first, the adversary (Erlik) is non-serpentine — Erlik is conceived in the Altaic / Turkic-Mongol religious tradition as an anthropomorphic underworld-ruler rather than as a serpent-or-dragon — and second, the opposition between Erlik and Ülgen is non-decisive — the dualism is a long-term cosmographic arrangement rather than a single combat-and-victory episode — with both features placing the material outside the operational definition of chaoskampf at scope.md §"Subject definition" (a sovereign or champion deity defeating a serpentine / monstrous adversary) on two structural grounds rather than one.footnote:[Harva 1938, *Die religiösen Vorstellungen der altaischen Völker*, FFC 125, on Erlik's non-serpentine anthropomorphic-underworld-ruler conception and on the non-decisive long-term-cosmographic-arrangement character of the Erlik-Ülgen opposition; Eliade 1964, *Shamanism: Archaic Techniques of Ecstasy*, on the same two distinctive structural features placing the material outside the chaoskampf operational definition; per scope.md §"Subject definition" on the operational definition of chaoskampf as a sovereign or champion deity defeating a serpentine / monstrous adversary; per inventory.approved.yaml notes on the erlik-ulgen entry registering the non-serpentine and non-decisive features as the inclusion-marginal grounds.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=harva-altaic-religious ; loc=Die religiösen Vorstellungen der altaischen Völker, on the Erlik / Ülgen pairing in the Altaic / Turkic-Mongol cosmological scheme

The Erlik / Ülgen pairing parallels the Indo-Iranian dualistic cosmology of the Avestan tradition (Ahura Mazdā as principal benevolent power versus Angra Mainyu as principal adversarial power, treated in chapter 13 of the present book within the Thraētaona / Aži Dahāka context) at the structural level of the high-god-and-adversarial-counterpart pairing, but does not inherit from the Indo-Iranian material and is not held to inherit from it in current Altaic-studies and history-of-religions scholarship: the structural parallel is registered as an independent typological convergence rather than as a transmission relationship, and the chapter's task is to register the parallel without claiming inheritance.footnote:[Harva 1938, *Die religiösen Vorstellungen der altaischen Völker*, FFC 125, on the structural parallel between the Erlik / Ülgen pairing and the Indo-Iranian Ahura Mazdā / Angra Mainyu dualistic cosmology at the level of the high-god-and-adversarial-counterpart pairing, on the absence of a transmission relationship between the Altaic and Indo-Iranian materials in current Altaic-studies and history-of-religions scholarship, and on the parallel as an independent typological convergence rather than a transmission relationship; Eliade 1964, *Shamanism: Archaic Techniques of Ecstasy*, on the same independent-typological-convergence framing; per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml cultural_relevance.cross_cultural_parallels on the Erlik / Ülgen parallel with Ahura Mazdā / Angra Mainyu and on the qualification that the Altaic version lacks the decisive combat that the Indo-Iranian material develops in the Frashokereti context.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=harva-altaic-religious ; loc=Die religiösen Vorstellungen der altaischen Völker, on the Erlik / Ülgen pairing in the Altaic / Turkic-Mongol cosmological scheme

The Erlik / Ülgen half is in the chapter inclusion-marginal in the strong sense: per the brief's special_instructions and per the chapter's classification as related-tier (family, "communal / non-slaying combat"), the Altaic dualism's two distinctive structural features (non-serpentine adversary; non-decisive opposition) place the material outside the operational definition of chaoskampf, and the chapter's task is to register the inclusion-marginal status explicitly and to characterise what the Erlik / Ülgen material adds to the book's category — namely, the registration of the dualistic-cosmology comparandum that is structurally adjacent to but distinct from chaoskampf proper, and the contribution of an independent (non-Indo-Iranian-derived) attestation of the high-god-and-adversarial-counterpart pairing to the comparative typology.footnote:[Per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml on the related-tier (family, "communal / non-slaying combat") classification of the chapter and on the brief's special_instructions emphasising the Erlik / Ülgen material as INCLUSION-MARGINAL on the non-serpentine-adversary and non-decisive-opposition grounds, and on the chapter's task of registering the dualistic-cosmology comparandum as structurally adjacent to but distinct from chaoskampf proper; per inventory.approved.yaml notes on the erlik-ulgen entry registering the inclusion-marginal status and the design decision (2026-04-23) to keep the entry RELATED with an explicit framing marker rather than moving to sidebar; per scope.md §"Subject definition" on the operational definition of chaoskampf and per scope.md §"Inclusion / exclusion criteria" on the related-tier criteria.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=eliade-shamanism ; loc=Shamanism: Archaic Techniques of Ecstasy, on the Erlik / Ülgen dualistic cosmology and Altaic-Siberian shamanic complex

The chapter's general comparative anchor for the long-arc combat-myth typology — Forsyth 1987's Sumer-through-Judeo-Christian-apocalyptic survey — is load-bearing for the Erlik / Ülgen half's inclusion-marginal characterisation as it is for the Bakunawa half's: the inclusion-marginal status of both halves depends on having a typology against which the marginality is characterised, and Forsyth supplies that long-arc typology even though Forsyth does not himself treat Philippine or Altaic content, with the chapter's use of the Forsyth citation being for the long-arc typological framing rather than for Philippine or Altaic-content scholarly coverage in either half.footnote:[Forsyth 1987, *The Old Enemy: Satan and the Combat Myth*, as the long-arc combat-myth typology anchor (Sumer through Judeo-Christian apocalyptic) load-bearing for the inclusion-marginal characterisation of both halves of the chapter; Forsyth 1987 does not itself treat Philippine or Altaic-content material and the chapter's use of the citation is for the long-arc typological framing rather than for Philippine or Altaic-content scholarly coverage; per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml sources.secondary entry on the Forsyth 1987 anchor with the not-for-Philippine-or-Altaic-content disclaimer and on the long-arc framing function for the chapter's typological work.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=forsyth-1987 ; loc=combat-myth long-arc typology (Sumer through Judeo-Christian apocalyptic)

=== Typology, area-specialist framing, and forward-links

[INFERENCE: the chapter's reading of both halves as inclusion-marginal cases that extend the present book's chaoskampf category at its outer boundaries — the Bakunawa cycle as the *communal / non-slaying* extension (where the serpent-and-human-community encounter is registered as a recurring communal-prophylactic practice rather than a hero-led decisive combat) and the Erlik / Ülgen pairing as the *dualistic-cosmology comparandum* extension (where the structural pattern of high-god-and-adversarial-counterpart pairing is structurally adjacent to but distinct from chaoskampf proper) — is a synthesis of the brief's special_instructions framing per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml, of Eugenio 2001's Philippine-folkloric framing for the Bakunawa half, of Eliade 1964's and Harva 1938's Altaic-religious-cosmology framing for the Erlik / Ülgen half, and of scope.md §"Subject definition" / §"Inclusion / exclusion criteria" / §"Appropriation protocol" on the operational definition of chaoskampf and the related-tier criteria. | basis: brief 30-bakunawa.yaml summary explicitly registers both halves as inclusion-marginal on different specific grounds (no hero-slaying for Bakunawa; non-serpentine adversary and non-decisive opposition for Erlik / Ülgen); brief special_instructions classifies the chapter as a principal contributor to Part II C8 (the refusal and the appropriation problem; ch. 41 of the present volume) on the chaoskampf-frame-applies-only-partially-at-best argument; Eugenio 2001's narrative-treatment apparatus develops the no-hero-slaying communal-noise-making feature at length; Harva 1938's and Eliade 1964's narrative-treatment apparatus develop the non-serpentine-and-non-decisive features at length; scope.md §"Subject definition" supplies the operational definition against which the inclusion-marginal characterisation operates. | risk: the inclusion-marginal characterisation is a comparativist framing that the area-specialist traditions for both halves do not adopt in their own register (Eugenio frames the Bakunawa material within Philippine eclipse-aetiology and communal-ritual; Eliade and Harva frame the Erlik / Ülgen material within Altaic-Siberian shamanic complex and dualistic religious cosmology); the chapter's task is to hold the inclusion-marginal characterisation as a comparativist heuristic that registers the area-specialist framing rather than as a tradition-internal characterisation, with the methodological discipline of the appropriation protocol at scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" point 3 applied to both halves of the chapter.]

The chapter's two halves stand to one another as parallel inclusion-marginal cases that nonetheless extend the present book's chaoskampf category in different specific respects: the Bakunawa half extends the comparative range to include the communal-prophylactic register where the serpent-and-human-community encounter is registered without a hero-figure, and the Erlik / Ülgen half registers the dualistic-cosmology comparandum that is structurally adjacent to but distinct from the chaoskampf-typology proper, with the parallel between the two halves being grounded in the inclusion-marginality itself rather than in any structural-narrative parallel between the Bakunawa narrative and the Erlik / Ülgen pairing as such.footnote:[Per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml on the chapter's pairing of the Bakunawa and Erlik / Ülgen halves under the "communal / non-slaying combat" theme rather than placing Erlik / Ülgen in the IE-reflexes chapter (19) per the toc.yaml deviation note, on the parallel between the two halves being grounded in the inclusion-marginality of both rather than in any structural-narrative parallel as such, and on the chapter's special_instructions emphasising that both halves are INCLUSION-MARGINAL for different reasons (no hero-slaying for Bakunawa; non-serpentine adversary and non-decisive opposition for Erlik / Ülgen) that nonetheless converge on the chapter's load-bearing methodological function as a contributor to Part II C8.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=eugenio-philippine-myths ; loc=Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths, sections on Bakunawa and the moons

[APPROPRIATION-FLAG: Philippine-folkloric and Altaic-religious-studies area specialists — for the Bakunawa half: Damiana L. Eugenio in *Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths* (Eugenio 2001) and her wider corpus of Philippine Folk Literature series compilations as the principal scholarly tradition reading the Bakunawa material within the Philippine-folkloric-and-eclipse-aetiology / communal-ritual register; for the Erlik / Ülgen half: Mircea Eliade in *Shamanism* (Eliade 1964), Uno Harva in *Die religiösen Vorstellungen der altaischen Völker* (Harva 1938), and the foundational Russian-Imperial ethnographic corpus of Grigory Potanin, Vasily Verbitsky, and Wilhelm Radloff (named in body prose per the named-but-flagged secondary-anchor pattern of Ch23 FC-02 / Ch26 FC-02 / Ch27 / Ch28, since none of Potanin, Verbitsky, or Radloff is currently a sources.yaml entry) as the principal scholarly tradition reading the Erlik / Ülgen material within the dualistic-cosmology / shamanic-complex register — read both halves of the chapter outside the chaos-vs-order combat register that the Gunkel-tradition chaoskampf typology presupposes, rather than within that combat register. The substance of the area-specialist objections differs across the two halves: for the Bakunawa half, Eugenio's framing within Philippine eclipse-aetiology and communal-ritual practice does not invoke the chaos-vs-order combat register at all and treats the Bakunawa cycle as a specific aetiological-and-communal-ritual practice within Visayan and Bikol communal life rather than as a member of any cross-cultural combat-myth category, with the area-specialist objection being to typological-assimilation of the cycle to the chaoskampf category on the load-bearing grounds that the cycle has no hero-figure and no decisive combat-and-victory episode (the structural feature on which the Eurasian combat-myth pattern depends); for the Erlik / Ülgen half, Eliade's framing within the Altaic-Siberian shamanic complex and Harva's framing within the systematic religious cosmology of the Altaic / Turkic-Mongol peoples both treat the Erlik / Ülgen pairing as a dualistic-cosmological structure within which shamanic ritual mediation is conducted rather than as a combat-and-victory episode, with the area-specialist objection being to typological-assimilation on the load-bearing grounds that the adversary (Erlik) is non-serpentine and that the opposition is non-decisive (the two structural features on which the Eurasian combat-myth pattern depends). The area-specialist objection-strength for both halves is therefore strong: each half of the chapter is inclusion-marginal precisely because the area-specialist tradition for that half does not place the material within the chaoskampf register at all, and each contributes a strong-objection case to the spectrum of objection-strengths that Part II C8 develops — with the Bakunawa half closer in objection-strength to the Ch32 Nanabozho refusal case (where the Hallowell-Johnston-T. Smith-Vizenor area-specialist tradition rejects combat-and-defeat-of-the-other framing in favour of necessary-counterpart-dialectic framing) than to the milder Ch26 Pariacaca and Ch27 Mapuche cases, and with the Erlik / Ülgen half representing a structurally distinctive strong-objection case in which the dualistic-cosmology framing and the chaoskampf typology converge on no structural feature at the load-bearing level. The chapter's load-bearing methodological move is to use the inclusion-marginal characterisation as the heuristic that registers exactly these objections: the Bakunawa cycle is recognised as featuring a serpentine adversary (and so is structurally adjacent to chaoskampf at one level) but is registered as inclusion-marginal on the no-hero-slaying ground, and the Erlik / Ülgen pairing is recognised as featuring a high-god-and-adversarial-counterpart structure (and so is structurally adjacent to chaoskampf at another level) but is registered as inclusion-marginal on the non-serpentine-and-non-decisive grounds. Forwarded to Part II C8 (refusal and appropriation problem; ch. 41 of the present volume) as PRINCIPAL CASES for the chaoskampf-frame-applies-only-partially-at-best argument per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml special_instructions; forwarded also to Part II C7 (inversion / transformation problem; ch. 40) as cases at the structurally-adjacent boundary of the chaoskampf category that test the typology's application range, with the inclusion-marginal characterisation distinct from the inversion characterisation of the Ch28 Bida case (where the chaoskampf structural features are present with valences reversed at every load-bearing point) and from the transformation characterisations of the Ch23 Susanoo and Ch26 Pariacaca cases (where the chaoskampf category transforms when the area-specialist account is taken as load-bearing). Eugenio is the chapter's principal anchor for the Bakunawa half and named in the first factual pass per scope.md §"Appropriation protocol" point 1; Eliade and Harva are the principal anchors for the Erlik / Ülgen half and similarly named in the first factual pass; Potanin, Verbitsky, and Radloff are named in body prose at the Source overview Erlik / Ülgen recording-corpus claim per the named-but-flagged secondary-anchor pattern (none currently in sources.yaml; deferred to inventory-audit per the Ch23 FC-02 / Ch26 FC-02 / Ch27 / Ch28 named-but-flagged precedent).]

The chapter's place within the present book's chaoskampf typology is — as the brief's "family (related-tier, 'communal / non-slaying combat')" classification specifies — a related-tier (rather than core-tier) family chapter that contributes the load-bearing structural extension-and-boundary-case material against which the chaoskampf typology's load-bearing assumptions can be tested at its outer boundaries; the chapter's structural significance for the present book is therefore disproportionate to its tier classification, since the related-tier classification (correct per scope.md §"Inclusion / exclusion criteria" because the chaoskampf-load-bearing functions are weaker or absent for both halves) is consistent with the chapter's load-bearing methodological function for Part II C8 as a principal contributor to the chaoskampf-frame-applies-only-partially-at-best argument.footnote:[Per scope.md §"Inclusion / exclusion criteria" on the related-tier criteria where the chaoskampf-load-bearing functions are weaker or absent; per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml chapter_type "family (related-tier, 'communal / non-slaying combat')" classification; per scope.md §"Book structure" Part II C8 on the chapter's load-bearing methodological function for the refusal and the appropriation problem comparative chapter (ch. 41 of the present volume); per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml special_instructions on the chapter's status as a PRINCIPAL CASE for the Part II C8 chaoskampf-frame-applies-only-partially-at-best argument.]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=eliade-shamanism ; loc=Shamanism: Archaic Techniques of Ecstasy, on the Erlik / Ülgen dualistic cosmology and Altaic-Siberian shamanic complex

The chapter's principal forward-link within the present book is to Part II C8 (the comparative chapter on the refusal and the appropriation problem, ch. 41 of the present volume) — where the present chapter contributes both the Bakunawa half and the Erlik / Ülgen half as PRINCIPAL CASES for the chaoskampf-frame-applies-only-partially-at-best argument, with the two halves between them representing the two specific inclusion-marginality grounds (no hero-slaying / communal-prophylactic register for Bakunawa; non-serpentine adversary and non-decisive opposition / dualistic-cosmology comparandum for Erlik / Ülgen) on which the chapter's contribution to C8 rests.footnote:[Per brief 30-bakunawa.yaml special_instructions on the chapter's contribution to Part II C8 as a PRINCIPAL CASE alongside the Ch23 Susanoo / Yamata no Orochi material, the Ch26 Pariacaca cycle, the Ch27 Mapuche Kai-Kai / Tren-Tren material, the Ch28 Bida / Wagadu material, the Ch29 Mwindo / Kirimu material, the Ch32 Nanabozho / Mishipeshu material, and the Rainbow Serpent material (folded into C8 per toc.yaml self_checks); per scope.md §"Book structure" Part II C8 on the refusal and the appropriation problem comparative chapter (ch. 41 of the present volume).]
// EVIDENCE: source_id=eugenio-philippine-myths ; loc=Philippine Folk Literature: The Myths, sections on Bakunawa and the moons
